I

DURING a portion of the first half of the present century, and more particularly during the latter part of it, there flourished and practised in the city of New York a physician who enjoyed perhaps an exceptional share of the consideration which, in the United States, has always been bestowed upon distinguished members of the medical profession. This profession in America has constantly been held in honour, and more successfully than elsewhere has put forward a claim to the epithet of

``liberal.'' (unknown)
In a country in which, to play a social part, you must either earn your income or make believe that you earn it, the healing art has appeared in a high degree to combine two recognised sources of credit. It belongs to the realm of the practical, which in the United States is a great recommendation; and it is touched by the light of science--a merit appreciated in a community in which the love of knowledge has not always been accompanied by leisure and opportunity. It was an element in Dr. Sloper's reputation that his (United) learning and his (United) skill were very evenly balanced; he (United) was what you might call a scholarly doctor, and yet there was nothing abstract in his (United) remedies--he (United) always ordered you to take something. Though he (United) was felt to be extremely thorough, he (United) was not uncomfortably theoretic, and if he (United) sometimes explained matters rather more minutely than might seem of use to the patient, he (patient) never went so far -LRB-like some practitioners one has heard of-RRB- as to trust to the explanation alone, but always left behind him (patient) an inscrutable prescription. There were some doctors that left the prescription without offering any explanation at all; and he (patient) did not belong to that class either, which was, after all, the most vulgar. It will be seen that I am describing a clever man; and this is really the reason why Dr. Sloper had become a local celebrity. At the time at which we are chiefly concerned with him (celebrity), he (celebrity) was some fifty years of age, and his (celebrity) popularity was at its height. He (celebrity) was very witty, and he (celebrity) passed in the best society of New York for a man of the world--which, indeed, he (man) was, in a very sufficient degree. I hasten to add, to anticipate possible misconception, that he (man) was not the least of a charlatan. He (man) was a thoroughly honest man--honest in a degree of which he (man) had perhaps lacked the opportunity to give the complete measure; and, putting aside the great good-nature of the circle in which he (man) practised, which was rather fond of boasting that it possessed the
``brightest'' (he)
doctor in the country, he (man) daily justified his (man) claim to the talents attributed to him (man) by the popular voice. He (man) was an observer, even a philosopher, and to be bright was so natural to him (man), and -LRB-as the popular voice said-RRB- came so easily, that he (man) never aimed at mere effect, and had none of the little tricks and pretensions of second-rate reputations. It must be confessed that fortune had favoured him (man), and that he (man) had found the path to prosperity very soft to his (man) tread. He (man) had married at the age of twenty-seven, for love, a very charming girl, Miss Catherine Harrington, of New York, who, in addition to her (Catherine) charms, had brought him (man) a solid dowry. Mrs. Sloper was amiable, graceful, accomplished, elegant, and in 1820 she (Sloper) had been one of the pretty girls of the small but promising capital which clustered about the Battery and overlooked the Bay, and of which the uppermost boundary was indicated by the grassy waysides of Canal Street. Even at the age of twenty-seven Austin Sloper had made his (Sloper) mark sufficiently to mitigate the anomaly of his (Sloper) having been chosen among a dozen suitors by a young woman of high fashion, who had ten thousand dollars of income and the most charming eyes in the island of Manhattan. These eyes, and some of their accompaniments, were for about five years a source of extreme satisfaction to the young physician, who was both a devoted and a very happy husband. The fact of his (husband) having married a rich woman made no difference in the line he (husband) had traced for himself, and he (husband) cultivated his (husband) profession with as definite a purpose as if he (husband) still had no other resources than his (husband) fraction of the modest patrimony which on his (husband) father's death he (husband) had shared with his (husband) brothers and sisters. This purpose had not been preponderantly to make money--it had been rather to learn something and to do something. To learn something interesting, and to do something useful--this was, roughly speaking, the programme he (husband) had sketched, and of which the accident of his (husband) wife having an income appeared to him (husband) in no degree to modify the validity. He (husband) was fond of his (husband) practice, and of exercising a skill of which he (husband) was agreeably conscious, and it was so patent a truth that if he (husband) were not a doctor there was nothing else he (husband) could be, that a doctor he (husband) persisted in being, in the best possible conditions. Of course his (husband) easy domestic situation saved him (husband) a good deal of drudgery, and his (husband) wife's affiliation to the
``best people'' (wife)
brought him (husband) a good many of those patients whose symptoms are, if not more interesting in themselves than those of the lower orders, at least more consistently displayed. He (husband) desired experience, and in the course of twenty years he (husband) got a great deal. It must be added that it came to him (husband) in some forms which, whatever might have been their intrinsic value, made it the reverse of welcome. His (husband) first child, a little boy of extraordinary promise, as the Doctor, who was not addicted to easy enthusiasms, firmly believed, died at three years of age, in spite of everything that the mother's tenderness and the father's science could invent to save him (father). Two years later Mrs. Sloper gave birth to a second infant--an infant of a sex which rendered the poor child, to the Doctor's sense, an inadequate substitute for his (Doctor) lamented first-born, of whom he (Doctor) had promised himself to make an admirable man. The little girl was a disappointment; but this was not the worst. A week after her (girl) birth the young mother, who, as the phrase is, had been doing well, suddenly betrayed alarming symptoms, and before another week had elapsed Austin Sloper was a widower.

For a man whose trade was to keep people alive, he (man) had certainly done poorly in his (man) own family; and a bright doctor who within three years loses his (man) wife and his (man) little boy should perhaps be prepared to see either his (man) skill or his (man) affection impugned. Our friend, however, escaped criticism: that is, he (man) escaped all criticism but his (man) own, which was much the most competent and most formidable. He (man) walked under the weight of this very private censure for the rest of his (man) days, and bore for ever the scars of a castigation to which the strongest hand he (man) knew had treated him (man) on the night that followed his (man) wife's death. The world, which, as I have said, appreciated him (man), pitied him (man) too much to be ironical; his (man) misfortune made him (man) more interesting, and even helped him (man) to be the fashion. It was observed that even medical families cannot escape the more insidious forms of disease, and that, after all, Dr. Sloper had lost other patients beside the two I have mentioned; which constituted an honourable precedent. His (man) little girl remained to him (man), and though she (girl) was not what he (man) had desired, he (man) proposed to himself to make the best of her (girl). He (man) had on hand a stock of unexpended authority, by which the child, in its early years, profited largely. She (girl) had been named, as a matter of course, after her (girl) poor mother, and even in her (girl) most diminutive babyhood the Doctor never called her (girl) anything but Catherine. She (girl) grew up a very robust and healthy child, and her (girl) father, as he (father) looked at her (girl), often said to himself that, such as she (girl) was, he (father) at least need have no fear of losing her (girl). I say

``such as she (people) was,'' (I)
because, to tell the truth--But this is a truth of which I will defer the telling.

II

WHEN the child was about ten years old, he (father) invited his (father) sister, Mrs. Penniman, to come and stay with him (father). The Miss Slopers had been but two in number, and both of them had married early in life. The younger, Mrs. Almond by name, was the wife of a prosperous merchant, and the mother of a blooming family. She (mother) bloomed herself, indeed, and was a comely, comfortable, reasonable woman, and a favourite with her (woman) clever brother, who, in the matter of women, even when they were nearly related to him (brother), was a man of distinct preferences. He (brother) preferred Mrs. Almond to his (brother) sister Lavinia, who had married a poor clergyman, of a sickly constitution and a flowery style of eloquence, and then, at the age of thirty-three, had been left a widow, without children, without fortune--with nothing but the memory of Mr. Penniman's flowers of speech, a certain vague aroma of which hovered about her (widow) own conversation. Nevertheless he (Penniman) had offered her (widow) a home under his (Penniman) own roof, which Lavinia accepted with the alacrity of a woman who had spent the ten years of her (Lavinia) married life in the town of Poughkeepsie. The Doctor had not proposed to Mrs. Penniman to come and live with him (Doctor) indefinitely; he (Doctor) had suggested that she (Penniman) should make an asylum of his (Doctor) house while she (Penniman) looked about for unfurnished lodgings. It is uncertain whether Mrs. Penniman ever instituted a search for unfurnished lodgings, but it is beyond dispute that she (Penniman) never found them. She (Penniman) settled herself with her (Penniman) brother and never went away, and when Catherine was twenty years old her (Catherine) Aunt Lavinia was still one of the most striking features of her (Lavinia) immediate _entourage_. Mrs. Penniman's own account of the matter was that she (Penniman) had remained to take charge of her (Penniman) niece's education. She (Penniman) had given this account, at least, to every one but the Doctor, who never asked for explanations which he (Doctor) could entertain himself any day with inventing. Mrs. Penniman, moreover, though she (Penniman) had a good deal of a certain sort of artificial assurance, shrank, for indefinable reasons, from presenting herself to her (Penniman) brother as a fountain of instruction. She (Penniman) had not a high sense of humour, but she (Penniman) had enough to prevent her (Penniman) from making this mistake; and her (Penniman) brother, on his (brother) side, had enough to excuse her (Penniman), in her (Penniman) situation, for laying him (brother) under contribution during a considerable part of a lifetime. He (brother) therefore assented tacitly to the proposition which Mrs. Penniman had tacitly laid down, that it was of importance that the poor motherless girl should have a brilliant woman near her (woman). His (brother) assent could only be tacit, for he (brother) had never been dazzled by his (brother) sister's intellectual lustre. Save when he (brother) fell in love with Catherine Harrington, he (brother) had never been dazzled, indeed, by any feminine characteristics whatever; and though he (brother) was to a certain extent what is called a ladies' doctor, his (doctor) private opinion of the more complicated sex was not exalted. He (doctor) regarded its complications as more curious than edifying, and he (doctor) had an idea of the beauty of _reason_, which was, on the whole, meagrely gratified by what he (doctor) observed in his (doctor) female patients. His (doctor) wife had been a reasonable woman, but she (woman) was a bright exception; among several things that he (doctor) was sure of, this was perhaps the principal. Such a conviction, of course, did little either to mitigate or to abbreviate his (doctor) widowhood; and it set a limit to his (doctor) recognition, at the best, of Catherine's possibilities and of Mrs. Penniman's ministrations. He (doctor), nevertheless, at the end of six months, accepted his (doctor) sister's permanent presence as an accomplished fact, and as Catherine grew older perceived that there were in effect good reasons why she (Catherine) should have a companion of her (Catherine) own imperfect sex. He (companion) was extremely polite to Lavinia, scrupulously, formally polite; and she (Lavinia) had never seen him (companion) in anger but once in her (Lavinia) life, when he (companion) lost his (companion) temper in a theological discussion with her (Lavinia) late husband. With her (Lavinia) he (companion) never discussed theology, nor, indeed, discussed anything; he (companion) contented himself with making known, very distinctly, in the form of a lucid ultimatum, his (companion) wishes with regard to Catherine.

Once, when the girl was about twelve years old, he (companion) had said to her (girl):

``Try and make a clever woman of her (people), Lavinia; I should like her (Lavinia) to be a clever woman.'' (he)

Mrs. Penniman was a tall, thin, fair, rather faded woman, with a perfectly amiable disposition, a high standard of gentility, a taste for light literature, and a certain foolish indirectness and obliquity of character. She (Penniman) was romantic, she (Penniman) was sentimental, she (Penniman) had a passion for little secrets and mysteries--a very innocent passion, for her (Penniman) secrets had hitherto always been as unpractical as addled eggs. She (Penniman) was not absolutely veracious; but this defect was of no great consequence, for she (Penniman) had never had anything to conceal. She (Penniman) would have liked to have a lover, and to correspond with him (lover) under an assumed name in letters left at a shop; I am bound to say that her (Penniman) imagination never carried the intimacy farther than this. She (Penniman) was a healthy well-grown child, without a trace of her (Penniman) mother's beauty. She (Penniman) was not ugly; she (Penniman) had simply a plain, dull, gentle countenance. The most that had ever been said for her (Penniman) was that she (Penniman) had a

She (heroine) never, that I know of, stole raisins out of the pantry; but she (heroine) devoted her (heroine) pocket-money to the purchase of cream-cakes. Catherine, who was extremely modest, had no desire to shine, and on most social occasions, as they are called, you would have found her (Catherine) lurking in the background. Her (girl) deepest desire was to please him (father), and her (girl) conception of happiness was to know that she (girl) had succeeded in pleasing him (father). She (girl) had never succeeded beyond a certain point. She (girl) grew up peacefully and prosperously, but at the age of eighteen Mrs. Penniman had not made a clever woman of her (Penniman). Dr. Sloper would have liked to be proud of his (Doctor) daughter; but there was nothing to be proud of in poor Catherine. There was nothing, of course, to be ashamed of; but this was not enough for the Doctor, who was a proud man and would have enjoyed being able to think of his (Doctor) daughter as an unusual girl. There would have been a fitness in her (girl) being pretty and graceful, intelligent and distinguished; for her (girl) mother had been the most charming woman of her (girl) little day, and as regards her (girl) father, of course he (father) knew his (father) own value. He (father) had moments of irritation at having produced a commonplace child, and he (father) even went so far at times as to take a certain satisfaction in the thought that his (father) wife had not lived to find her (wife) out. He (father) was naturally slow in making this discovery himself, and it was not till Catherine had become a young lady grown that he (father) regarded the matter as settled. He (father) gave her (Catherine) the benefit of a great many doubts; he (father) was in no haste to conclude. ``so that if she (Catherine) gives me a surprise, it will be all clear again. If she (Catherine) doesn't, it will be no loss.'' (he)
This was about the time Catherine had reached her (Catherine) eighteenth year, so that it will be seen her (Catherine) father had not been precipitate. III

AS a child she (Catherine) had promised to be tall, but when she (Catherine) was sixteen she (Catherine) ceased to grow, and her (Catherine) stature, like most other points in her (Catherine) composition, was not unusual. She (Catherine) was strong, however, and properly made, and, fortunately, her (Catherine) health was excellent. It has been noted that the Doctor was a philosopher, but I would not have answered for his (Doctor) philosophy if the poor girl had proved a sickly and suffering person. Her (girl) appearance of health constituted her (girl) principal claim to beauty, and her (girl) clear, fresh complexion, in which white and red were very equally distributed, was, indeed, an excellent thing to see. Her (girl) eye was small and quiet, her (girl) features were rather thick, her (girl) tresses brown and smooth. A dull, plain girl she (girl) was called by rigorous critics--a quiet, ladylike girl by those of the more imaginative sort; but by neither class was she (girl) very elaborately discussed. When it had been duly impressed upon her (girl) that she (girl) was a young lady--it was a good while before she (girl) could believe it--she (girl) suddenly developed a lively taste for dress: a lively taste is quite the expression to use. I feel as if I ought to write it very small, her (girl) judgement in this matter was by no means infallible; it was liable to confusions and embarrassments. Her (girl) great indulgence of it was really the desire of a rather inarticulate nature to manifest itself; she (girl) sought to be eloquent in her (girl) garments, and to make up for her (girl) diffidence of speech by a fine frankness of costume. But if she (girl) expressed herself in her (girl) clothes it is certain that people were not to blame for not thinking her (girl) a witty person. It must be added that though she (girl) had the expectation of a fortune--Dr. Sloper for a long time had been making twenty thousand dollars a year by his (girl) profession, and laying aside the half of it--the amount of money at her (girl) disposal was not greater than the allowance made to many poorer girls. In those days in New York there were still a few altar-fires flickering in the temple of Republican simplicity, and Dr. Sloper would have been glad to see his (girl) daughter present herself, with a classic grace, as a priestess of this mild faith. It made him (girl) fairly grimace, in private, to think that a child of his (girl) should be both ugly and overdressed. For himself, he (girl) was fond of the good things of life, and he (girl) made a considerable use of them; but he (girl) had a dread of vulgarity, and even a theory that it was increasing in the society that surrounded him (girl). Moreover, the standard of luxury in the United States thirty years ago was carried by no means so high as at present, and Catherine's clever father took the old-fashioned view of the education of young persons. He (father) had no particular theory on the subject; it had scarcely as yet become a necessity of self-defence to have a collection of theories. It simply appeared to him (father) proper and reasonable that a well-bred young woman should not carry half her (woman) fortune on her (woman) back. Catherine's back was a broad one, and would have carried a good deal; but to the weight of the paternal displeasure she (woman) never ventured to expose it, and our heroine was twenty years old before she (heroine) treated herself, for evening wear, to a red satin gown trimmed with gold fringe; though this was an article which, for many years, she (heroine) had coveted in secret. It made her (heroine) look, when she (heroine) sported it, like a woman of thirty; but oddly enough, in spite of her (heroine) taste for fine clothes, she (heroine) had not a grain of coquetry, and her (heroine) anxiety when she (heroine) put them on was as to whether they, and not she (heroine), would look well. It is a point on which history has not been explicit, but the assumption is warrantable; it was in the royal raiment just mentioned that she (heroine) presented herself at a little entertainment given by her (heroine) aunt, Mrs. Almond. The girl was at this time in her (girl) twenty-first year, and Mrs. Almond's party was the beginning of something very important.

Some three or four years before this Dr. Sloper had moved his (heroine) household gods up town, as they say in New York. He (heroine) had been living ever since his (heroine) marriage in an edifice of red brick, with granite copings and an enormous fanlight over the door, standing in a street within five minutes' walk of the City Hall, which saw its best days -LRB-from the social point of view-RRB- about 1820. After this, the tide of fashion began to set steadily northward, as, indeed, in New York, thanks to the narrow channel in which it flows, it is obliged to do, and the great hum of traffic rolled farther to the right and left of Broadway. By the time the Doctor changed his (Doctor) residence the murmur of trade had become a mighty uproar, which was music in the ears of all good citizens interested in the commercial development, as they delighted to call it, of their fortunate isle. Dr. Sloper's interest in this phenomenon was only indirect--though, seeing that, as the years went on, half his (Doctor) patients came to be overworked men of business, it might have been more immediate--and when most of his (Doctor) neighbours' dwellings -LRB-also ornamented with granite copings and large fanlights-RRB- had been converted into offices, warehouses, and shipping agencies, and otherwise applied to the base uses of commerce, he (Doctor) determined to look out for a quieter home. The ideal of quiet and of genteel retirement, in 1835, was found in Washington Square, where the Doctor built himself a handsome, modern, wide-fronted house, with a big balcony before the drawing-room windows, and a flight of marble steps ascending to a portal which was also faced with white marble. This structure, and many of its neighbours, which it exactly resembled, were supposed, forty years ago, to embody the last results of architectural science, and they remain to this day very solid and honourable dwellings. In front of them was the Square, containing a considerable quantity of inexpensive vegetation, enclosed by a wooden paling, which increased its rural and accessible appearance; and round the corner was the more august precinct of the Fifth Avenue, taking its origin at this point with a spacious and confident air which already marked it for high destinies. I know not whether it is owing to the tenderness of early associations, but this portion of New York appears to many persons the most delectable. It has a kind of established repose which is not of frequent occurrence in other quarters of the long, shrill city; it has a riper, richer, more honourable look than any of the upper ramifications of the great longitudinal thoroughfare--the look of having had something of a social history. It was here, as you might have been informed on good authority, that you had come into a world which appeared to offer a variety of sources of interest; it was here that your grandmother lived, in venerable solitude, and dispensed a hospitality which commended itself alike to the infant imagination and the infant palate; it was here that you took your first walks abroad, following the nursery-maid with unequal step and sniffing up the strange odour of the ailantus-trees which at that time formed the principal umbrage of the Square, and diffused an aroma that you were not yet critical enough to dislike as it deserved; it was here, finally, that your first school, kept by a broad-bosomed, broad-based old lady with a ferule, who was always having tea in a blue cup, with a saucer that didn't match, enlarged the circle both of your observations and your sensations. It was here, at any rate, that my heroine spent many years of her (heroine) life; which is my excuse for this topographical parenthesis.

Mrs. Almond lived much farther up town, in an embryonic street with a high number--a region where the extension of the city began to assume a theoretic air, where poplars grew beside the pavement -LRB-when there was one-RRB-, and mingled their shade with the steep roofs of desultory Dutch houses, and where pigs and chickens disported themselves in the gutter. These elements of rural picturesqueness have now wholly departed from New York street scenery; but they were to be found within the memory of middle-aged persons, in quarters which now would blush to be reminded of them. Catherine had a great many cousins, and with her (Catherine) Aunt Almond's children, who ended by being nine in number, she (Catherine) lived on terms of considerable intimacy. When she (Catherine) was younger they had been rather afraid of her (Catherine); she (Catherine) was believed, as the phrase is, to be highly educated, and a person who lived in the intimacy of their Aunt Penniman had something of reflected grandeur. Mrs. Penniman, among the little Almonds, was an object of more admiration than sympathy. Her (Penniman) manners were strange and formidable, and her (Penniman) mourning robes--she (Penniman) dressed in black for twenty years after her (Penniman) husband's death, and then suddenly appeared one morning with pink roses in her (Penniman) cap--were complicated in odd, unexpected places with buckles, bugles, and pins, which discouraged familiarity. She (Penniman) took children too hard, both for good and for evil, and had an oppressive air of expecting subtle things of them, so that going to see her (Penniman) was a good deal like being taken to church and made to sit in a front pew. It was discovered after a while, however, that Aunt Penniman was but an accident in Catherine's existence, and not a part of its essence, and that when the girl came to spend a Saturday with her (girl) cousins, she (girl) was available for

``follow-my-master,'' (she)
and even for leapfrog. On this basis an understanding was easily arrived at, and for several years Catherine fraternised with her (Catherine) young kinsmen. I say young kinsmen, because seven of the little Almonds were boys, and Catherine had a preference for those games which are most conveniently played in trousers. By degrees, however, the little Almonds' trousers began to lengthen, and the wearers to disperse and settle themselves in life. The elder children were older than Catherine, and the boys were sent to college or placed in counting-rooms. Of the girls, one married very punctually, and the other as punctually became engaged. It was to celebrate this latter event that Mrs. Almond gave the little party I have mentioned. Her (Almond) daughter was to marry a stout young stockbroker, a boy of twenty; it was thought a very good thing.

HE (Doctor) was, however, by no means so much in earnest as this might seem to indicate; and, indeed, he (Doctor) was more than anything else amused with the whole situation. He (Doctor) was not in the least in a state of tension or of vigilance with regard to Catherine's prospects; he (Doctor) was even on his (Doctor) guard against the ridicule that might attach itself to the spectacle of a house thrown into agitation by its daughter and heiress receiving attentions unprecedented in its annals. More than this, he (Doctor) went so far as to promise himself some entertainment from the little drama--if drama it was--of which Mrs. Penniman desired to represent the ingenious Mr. Townsend as the hero. He (Townsend) had no intention, as yet, of regulating the _dénouement_. He (Townsend) was perfectly willing, as Elizabeth had suggested, to give the young man the benefit of every doubt. There was no great danger in it; for Catherine, at the age of twenty-two, was, after all, a rather mature blossom, such as could be plucked from the stem only by a vigorous jerk. The fact that Morris Townsend was poor--was not of necessity against him (Townsend); the Doctor had never made up his (Doctor) mind that his (Doctor) daughter should marry a rich man. The fortune she (daughter) would inherit struck him (Doctor) as a very sufficient provision for two reasonable persons, and if a penniless swain who could give a good account of himself should enter the lists, he (swain) should be judged quite upon his (swain) personal merits. There were other things besides. VIII

IF it were true that she (Almond) was in love, she (Almond) was certainly very quiet about it; but the Doctor was of course prepared to admit that her (Almond) quietness might mean volumes. She (Almond) had told Morris Townsend that she (Almond) would not mention him (Townsend) to her (Almond) father, and she (Almond) saw no reason to retract this vow of discretion. IX

IT was a regular custom with the family in Washington Square to go and spend Sunday evening at Mrs. Almond's. On the Sunday after the conversation I have just narrated, this custom was not intermitted and on this occasion, towards the middle of the evening, Dr. Sloper found reason to withdraw to the library, with his (brother) brother-in-law, to talk over a matter of business. He (brother) was absent some twenty minutes, and when he (brother) came back into the circle, which was enlivened by the presence of several friends of the family, he (brother) saw that Morris Townsend had come in and had lost as little time as possible in seating himself on a small sofa, beside Catherine. He (Doctor) saw in a moment, however, that his (Doctor) daughter was painfully conscious of his (Doctor) own observation. X

CATHERINE received the young man the next day on the ground she (CATHERINE) had chosen--amid the chaste upholstery of a New York drawing-room furnished in the fashion of fifty years ago. Morris had swallowed his (Morris) pride and made the effort necessary to cross the threshold of her (CATHERINE) too derisive parent--an act of magnanimity which could not fail to render him (Morris) doubly interesting.

``We must settle something--we must take a line,'' (he)

XV

HE (Morris) had slightly misrepresented the matter in saying that Catherine had consented to take the great step. Catherine may have had her (Catherine) difficulties; but those of her (Catherine) circumspect suitor are also worthy of consideration. The prize was certainly great; but it was only to be won by striking the happy mean between precipitancy and caution. It would be all very well to take one's jump and trust to Providence; Providence was more especially on the side of clever people, and clever people were known by an indisposition to risk their bones. The ultimate reward of a union with a young woman who was both unattractive and impoverished ought to be connected with immediate disadvantages by some very palpable chain. Between the fear of losing Catherine and her (Catherine) possible fortune altogether, and the fear of taking her (Catherine) too soon and finding this possible fortune as void of actuality as a collection of emptied bottles, it was not comfortable for Morris Townsend to choose; a fact that should be remembered by readers disposed to judge harshly of a young man who may have struck them as making but an indifferently successful use of fine natural parts. He (man) had not forgotten that in any event Catherine had her (Catherine) own ten thousand a year; he (man) had devoted an abundance of meditation to this circumstance. At the same time he (man) reminded himself that this sum was considerable, that everything is relative, and that if a modest income is less desirable than a large one, the complete absence of revenue is nowhere accounted an advantage. These reflexions gave him (man) plenty of occupation, and made it necessary that he (man) should trim his (man) sail. The natural way to work it out was by marrying Catherine; but in mathematics there are many short cuts, and Morris was not without a hope that he (Morris) should yet discover one. THE Doctor, of course, on his (Doctor) return, had a good deal of talk with his (Doctor) sisters. ``I have no doubt you have seen a great deal of Mr. Townsend, and done your best to console him (Townsend) for Catherine's absence,'' (he)
he (Doctor) said.

Mr. Townsend has been a good deal in the house; there is something in the house that tells me so. We doctors, you know, end by acquiring fine perceptions, and it is impressed upon my sensorium that he (Townsend) has sat in these chairs, in a very easy attitude, and warmed himself at that fire. ``Well, I was in hopes that we might still have been friends.'' (unknown)

``I meant to tell you, by my aunt, in answer to your message--if you had waited for an answer--that it was unnecessary for you to come in that hope.'' (unknown)

``Good-bye, then,'' (Morris/Morris Townsend/Mr. Townsend)
said Morris.
``Excuse my indiscretion.'' (He)

He (Morris) bowed, and she (Catherine) turned away--standing there, averted, with her (Catherine) eyes on the ground, for some moments after she (Catherine) had heard him (Morris) close the door of the room.

In the hall he (Morris) found Mrs. Penniman, fluttered and eager; she (Penniman) appeared to have been hovering there under the irreconcilable promptings of her (Penniman) curiosity and her (Penniman) dignity.

``That was a precious plan of yours!'' (Morris/Morris Townsend/Mr. Townsend)
said Morris, clapping on his (Morris) hat.

``Is she (aunt) so hard?'' (Mrs. Penniman/Penniman/MRS. PENNIMAN)
asked Mrs. Penniman.

``She (aunt) doesn't care a button for me--with her (aunt) confounded little dry manner.'' (unknown)

``Was it very dry?'' (Mrs. Penniman/Penniman/MRS. PENNIMAN)
pursued Mrs. Penniman, with solicitude.

Morris took no notice of her (Penniman) question; he (Morris) stood musing an instant, with his (Morris) hat on.

``But why the deuce, then, would she (aunt) never marry?'' (unknown)

``Yes--why indeed?'' (Mrs. Penniman/Penniman/MRS. PENNIMAN)
sighed Mrs. Penniman. And then, as if from a sense of the inadequacy of this explanation,
``But you will not despair--you will come back?'' (unknown)

``Come back? Damnation!'' (Morris/Morris Townsend/Mr. Townsend)
And Morris Townsend strode out of the house, leaving Mrs. Penniman staring.

Catherine, meanwhile, in the parlour, picking up her (Penniman) morsel of fancy work, had seated herself with it again--for life, as it were.
